<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-quality genome of Eragrostis curvula grass provides insights into Poaceae evolution and supports new strategies to enhance forage quality"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2019 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-07-15"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The Poaceae constitute a taxon of flowering plants (grasses) that cover almost all Earth&#8217;s inhabitable range and comprises some of the genera most commonly used for human and animal nutrition. Many of these crops have been sequenced, like rice, Brachypodium, maize and, more recently, wheat. Some important members are still considered orphan crops, lacking a sequenced genome, but having important traits that make them attractive for sequencing. Among these traits is apomixis, clonal reproduction by seeds, present in some members of the Poaceae like Eragrostis curvula. A de novo, high-quality genome assembly and annotation for E. curvula have been obtained by sequencing 602&#8201;Mb of a diploid genotype using a strategy that combined long-read length sequencing with chromosome conformation capture. The scaffold N50 for this assembly was 43.41&#8201;Mb and the annotation yielded 56,469 genes. The availability of this genome assembly has allowed us to identify regions associated with forage quality and to develop strategies to sequence and assemble the complex tetraploid genotypes which harbor the apomixis control region(s). Understanding and subsequently manipulating the genetic drivers underlying apomixis could revolutionize agriculture."/>

Many of these crops have been sequenced, like rice, Brachypodium, maize and, more recently, wheat. The availability of this genome assembly has allowed us to identify regions associated with forage quality and to develop strategies to sequence and assemble the complex tetraploid genotypes which harbor the apomixis control region(s).             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-2585-5649"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-2585-5649</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="UniversitÃ  degli Studi di Perugia, Dip. di Scienze Agrarie, Alimentari e Ambientali" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1757 3630, grid.9027.c, UniversitÃ  degli Studi di Perugia, Dip. di Scienze Agrarie, Alimentari e Ambientali, Borgo XX Giugno 74, 06121, Perugia, Italy" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-M_-Caccamo" data-author-popup="auth-M_-Caccamo" data-corresp-id="c1">M. Echenique<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide">Â 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3755-9062"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-3755-9062</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Universidad Nacional del Sur" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2167 9444, grid.412236.0, Centro de Recursos Naturales Renovables de la Zona SemiÃ¡rida (CERZOS â CCT â CONICET BahÃ­a Blanca) and Departamento de AgronomÃ­a, Universidad Nacional del Sur, Camino de la Carrindanga km 7, 8000, BahÃ­a Blanca, Argentina" /></span></sup>Â </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The Poaceae constitute a taxon of flowering plants (grasses) that cover almost all Earthâs inhabitable range and comprises some of the genera most commonly used for human and animal nutrition. Many of these crops have been sequenced, like rice, Brachypodium, maize and, more recently, wheat. Some important members are still considered orphan crops, lacking a sequenced genome, but having important traits that make them attractive for sequencing. Among these traits is apomixis, clonal reproduction by seeds, present in some members of the Poaceae like <i>Eragrostis curvula</i>. A <i>de novo</i>, high-quality genome assembly and annotation for <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> have been obtained by sequencing 602âMb of a diploid genotype using a strategy that combined long-read length sequencing with chromosome conformation capture. The scaffold N50 for this assembly was 43.41âMb and the annotation yielded 56,469 genes. The availability of this genome assembly has allowed us to identify regions associated with forage quality and to develop strategies to sequence and assemble the complex tetraploid genotypes which harbor the apomixis control region(s). Understanding and subsequently manipulating the genetic drivers underlying apomixis could revolutionize agriculture.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Climate change modeling predicts sustained elevated temperatures in which C4 grasses will thrive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Morgan, J. A. et al. C4 grasses prosper as carbon dioxide eliminates desiccation in warmed semi-arid grassland. Nature. 476, 202â205 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d60274e653">1</a></sup>. <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> (Schrad.) Nees (weeping lovegrass) is a C4 perennial grass member of the Poaceae family, Chloridoideae subfamily. The <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> complex has a basic chromosome number of Xâ=â10 and includes cytotypes with different ploidy levels (from 2X to 8X) that may undergo sexual reproduction and facultative or obligate apomixis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Voigt, P. W. &amp; Bashaw, E. C. Facultative Apomixis in Eragrostis curvula. Crop Sci. 16, 803â806 (1976)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d60274e670">2</a></sup>. Its drought tolerance and capacity to grow in sandy soils make it highly valued, especially for cattle feed in semiarid regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Farrington, P. The seasonal growth of lovegrass (Eragrostis curvula) on deep sandy soils in a semi-arid environment. Aust j exp agr. 13, 383â388 (1973)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d60274e674">3</a></sup>. However, weeping lovegrass, like other C4 species, has lower nutritional quality compared to C3 species. Different molecular strategies have been developed in order to increase forage quality. Recently<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Saleme, M. D. et al. Silencing CAFFEOYL SHIKIMATE ESTERASE affects lignification and improves saccharification. Plant physiol. 175, 1040â1057 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d60274e678">4</a></sup>, the genes for class I and class II caffeoyl shikimate esterase (CSE) have been discovered to be involved in lignin regulation being interesting targets to improve forage quality through genetic engineering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Spangenberg, G. et al. Breeding Forage Plants in the Genome Era. In Molecular Breeding of Forage Crops 1â39 (Lorne and Hamilton, 2001)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d60274e682">5</a></sup>. In addition, <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> has been suggested as a potential biofuel crop<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Lauriault, L. M. et al. A Screening for Biofuel Feedstock Quality of Perennial Warm-Season Grasses in Semiarid Subtropical Environments. Res Rep New Mexico Agric Exp Sta. 774, 1â8 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d60274e693">6</a></sup>. In this context, the availability of a high-quality genome assembly for weeping lovegrass is essential to enable genetic improvement that aims to increase its digestibility and energy provision. Moreover, since <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> is a species adapted to high temperature, high radiation and drought, the characterization of the WRKY transcription factors could be central to understand the mechanism involved in resilience in case of environmental stresses.</p><p><i>Eragrostis</i> is a poorly studied polyphyletic genus, with more than 400 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="RoodtâWilding, R. &amp; Spies, J. J. Phylogenetic relationships in southern African chloridoid grasses (Poaceae) based on nuclear and chloroplast sequence data. Syst Biodivers. 4, 401â415 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d60274e708">7</a></sup>, originating from Africa and now distributed in tropical and mid warm-season regions all over the world. <i>Eragrostis tef</i>, a cereal from Ethiopia, and <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i>, a forage grass from the south of Africa, are the best-studied species of the genus. <i>Setaria italica</i> and <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> genomes were reported to be the closest relatives of <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cannarozzi, G. et al. Genome and transcriptome sequencing identifies breeding targets in the orphan crop tef (Eragrostis tef). BMC genomics. 15, 581, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2164-15-581&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d60274e733">8</a></sup>. However, comparisons of the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> transcriptome with the <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> genome and transcriptome sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Garbus, I. et al. De novo transcriptome sequencing and assembly from apomictic and sexual Eragrostis curvula genotypes. PloS one. 12, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0185595&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d60274e750">9</a></sup> support the involvement of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> in <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> evolution. Like <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kamies, R., Farrant, J. M., Tadele, Z., Cannarozzi, G. &amp; Rafudeen, M. S. A proteomic approach to investigate the drought response in the orphan crop Eragrostis tef. Proteomes. 15, 32, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.3390/proteomes5040032&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d60274e772">10</a></sup>, <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> is classified as an orphan, or underutilized, crop and despite its importance, very little research emphasis has been given to this species.</p><p>Until recently, the major limitation in any genome assembly project was given by the short length of the reads obtained<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiao, W. B. &amp; Schneeberger, K. The impact of third generation genomic technologies on plant genome assembly. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 36, 64â70 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d60274e785">11</a></sup>. The advent of new platforms for long molecule sequencing, such as the PacBio Sequel System and Oxford Nanopore Technologies systems, has greatly contributed in overcoming this limitation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nakano, K. et al. Advantages of genome sequencing by long-read sequencer using SMRT technology in medical area. Hum Cell. 30, 149â161 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d60274e789">12</a></sup>. The former is ideal for sequencing large genomes and provides high-quality long reads that allow genome reconstruction with an accuracy of more than 99.99%<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat Biotechnol 33, 623â630 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d60274e793">13</a></sup>. High-quality assemblies based on PacBio sequences were recently published for <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (135âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat Biotechnol 33, 623â630 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d60274e800">13</a></sup>, <i>Oropetium thomaeum</i> (245âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature. 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d60274e808">14</a></sup>, <i>Utricularia gibba</i> (82âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Lan, T. et al. Long-read sequencing uncovers the adaptive topography of a carnivorous plant genome. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 114, 4435â4441 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d60274e815">15</a></sup>, <i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> (1500âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jarvis, D. E. et al. The genome of Chenopodium quinoa. Nature. 542, 307â301 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d60274e822">16</a></sup>, <i>Zea mays</i> (2300âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature. 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d60274e830">17</a></sup> and <i>Helianthus annuus</i> (3000âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature. 546, 148â152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d60274e837">18</a></sup>. However, to build chromosome-level genome assemblies, spanning long genomic distances and to order the contigs in the right orientation it is necessary to complement the PacBio system with other technologies. Chicago<sup>Â®</sup> and Dovetailâ¢ Hi-C are two recently developed methodologies based on proximity DNA and chromatin ligation that complement the PacBio system and result in an extremely precise sequence assembly, orientating the contigs and increasing the N50 to values that can be as large as 30âMb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342â350 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d60274e843">19</a></sup>. A good example of this integration of technologies is shown by the genome assembly of the tropical fruit, the durian, <i>Durio zibethinus</i>, where the N50 was 22.7âMb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Teh, B. T. et al. The draft genome of tropical fruit durian (Durio zibethinus). Nat genet. 46, 1633â1641 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d60274e851">20</a></sup>.</p><p>The availability of a high-quality diploid genome assembly of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> will contribute to establishing its evolutionary relationships with other members of the Poaceae family, unraveling the taxonomy of the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> complex, looking for new strategies to improve forage quality and directing the assembly of more complex and heterozygous tetraploid genomes harboring the apomixis control region(s). The elucidation of this region(s) and the associated apomixis genes could lead to revolutionary developments in terms of crop improvement.</p><p>To obtain a high-quality genome assembly of the diploid <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genotype a combination of PacBio long read sequencing with Chicago and Hi-C technology was employed. The final genome assembly had 603âMb distributed in 1,143 scaffolds, an N50 of 43.4âMb with 28% of the bases corresponding to repetitive elements and was validated using DArT-seq and Simple sequence repeats (SSR) markers.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and Assembly</h3><p>The PacBio Sequel platform was employed to sequence two <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> libraries of 10 and 20âkb resulting in 6,223,627 and 3,309,811 raw reads respectively. The average read length size was 5,296 for the 10âkb and 7,018 for the 20âkb libraries, covering 90.32X of the estimated genome size. The best assembly was obtained using the FALCON software with the following settings: i) a length cut-off of 7,500âbp, ii) overlap filtering parameters of minimum and maximum coverage of 5 and 120X, respectively and iii) a maximum difference of coverage of 120X between the 5â² and 3â² ends. The <i>de novo</i> assembly consisted of 3,118 contigs with an N50 of 378,697âbp, representing 97% of the genome length (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Tab1">1</a>). This assembly was later polished using the software Arrow, improving the accuracy per base content and increasing the percentage of complete BUSCO genes from 88.4% to 96% (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Tab1">1</a>). In this assembly 98.88% of the bases were found with a quality score more than 30, this means that 98.88% of the polished genome has an error rate less than 0.001.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly metrics.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The next step was to sequence the Chicago library, achieving 432 million 2âÃâ150 paired-end raw reads. The combination of these reads with the primary contigs of the FALCON assembly using the Hi-Rise assembler increased the N50 from 0.378âMb to 0.791âMb, decreasing the number of sequences to 1,884 (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Tab1">1</a>). Although this strategy vastly improved the contiguity of the assembly, another improvement involved the preparation of a Hi-C library, in which 333 million 2âÃâ150 bp paired-end raw reads were achieved. Using the Hi-C library as input within the Chicago assembly the N50 increased to 43.41âMb and the number of scaffolds decreased to 1,143. The final BUSCO results were 96.4%, 80.3% of them were single copy while 16.1% were duplicated.</p><p>The pipeline used to sequence and assembly the genome, and the experimental steps in its analysis, are shown in Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S1</a>.</p><p>The <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly presented here is one of the few published genomes (NCBI Bioproject PRJNA508722) with a scaffold N50 value greater than 10âMb, having seven scaffolds with almost the size of complete chromosomes. A high level of contiguity was obtained, since 83.7% of the genome size was contained in the first 14 scaffolds (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="662" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Circos plot of the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly. (<b>A</b>) The fourteen longest scaffolds plus one scaffold representing the shortest scaffolds were merged. (<b>B</b>) Gene density, (<b>C</b>) Repeat elements content, (<b>D</b>) DArT reads density and (<b>E</b>) DArT marker density.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">DArT-seq (Diversity Arrays Technology sequenciation) Analyses and Marker Mapping</h3><p>DArT is a recently developed technology for SNP markers discovery based on the reduction of genome complexity. A total of 6,027 (95.5%) of the original 6,307 SNPs markers sequences present in cv. Victoria were successfully aligned to the genome assembly. The longest scaffolds present a proportionally higher number of markers than the shortest ones and the same tendency was appreciated in the gene number (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S1</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="322" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Regression between number of gene models (light blue circles) and number of DArT markers (green circles) and the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> scaffold length. Each circle represents a scaffold. The regression analysis shows that gene and DArT marker counts Â are directly proportional to Â theÂ scaffold size, meaning thatÂ the largest scaffold, the higher the gene and DArT count.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>DArT reads density in the chromosome-scale scaffolds was used to validate the assembly. DArT libraries are designed to avoid repetitive elements and target active regions covered predominantly by genes. A higher number of genes is expected in the distal arms regions than in the central regions. In fact, the seven longest scaffolds showed higher read density at the 3â² and 5â² ends than in the central region (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S2</a>). Thus, contigs with the longest length, the greatest number of genes and DArT markers could be complete chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Repetitive Elements and Gene Annotation</h3><p>The analyses over the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly established that 28.7% is composed by repetitive elements, mainly Long Terminal Repeats retroelements (LTR-RT) (16.97%), followed by DNA and unclassified elements (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S2</a>), as seen in most of the grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Estep, M. C., DeBarry, J. D. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. The dynamics of LTR retrotransposon accumulation across 25 million years of panicoid grass evolution. Heredity. 110, 194â204 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d60274e1239">21</a></sup>. Within the most representative LTR-RTs were the <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> superfamilies, accounting for 13.62% and 3.14% of the total, respectively, with the ratio between them of 4.3:1. This value is very close to the one found in <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> (4.27:1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Gebre, Y. G., Bertolini, E., PÃ¨, M. E. &amp; Zuccolo, A. Identification and characterization of abundant repetitive sequences in Eragrostis tef cv. Enatite genome. BMC Plant Biol. 16, 39, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/s12870-016-0725-4&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d60274e1256">22</a></sup> and higher than the corresponding one in <i>S</i>. <i>italica</i> (3.08:1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d60274e1267">23</a></sup>, <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i> (1.91:1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature. 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d60274e1277">17</a></sup> and <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i> (3.67:1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d60274e1288">24</a></sup> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S3</a>). Previously reported ESTs related to repetitive elements present in <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i>Â´s floral and leaf libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Romero, J., Selva, J. P., Pessino, S., Echenique, V. &amp; Garbus, I. Repetitive sequences in Eragrostis curvula cDNA EST libraries obtained from genotypes with different ploidy. Biol Plant. 60, 55â67 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d60274e1301">25</a></sup> were mapped onto the genome assembly. Using this scheme a LTR structure of a <i>Gypsy</i> superfamily of retrotransposons identified from theÂ EST EH191456 was validated, since it was present in at least 16 scaffolds (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="856" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Percentages of repetitive elements present in the <i>Eragrostis curvula</i> genome assembly. Each color represents a different class of element. The total content of repetitive elements present in the assembly was 28.7%.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Gene annotation was performed using an <i>ab initio</i> prediction algorithm combined with data from ESTs and RNA-seq databases from different tissues of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and from proteins of related species. After three iterations of the MAKER software, 56,469 gene models were obtained with an average size of 1,424âbp and 93.4% of the complete BUSCO genes (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S3</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S5</a>). These genes were classified into two main categories: High Confidence (HC) and Low Confidence (LC) genes, then divided into two and three subcategories, respectively. Using this strategy 13,376 HC genes and 20,330 LC1 genes were identified, representing approximately the number of genes expected for the species (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig4">4</a>). The protein domains were inferred using the InterProScan software (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S6</a>), finding 35,713 matches in the Pfam domain database. Gene ontology analysis based on 56,469 genes classified 33,601 genes into biological processes, 17,710 into cellular components and 33,820 into molecular function, finding 29,462 genes with at least one GO annotation category (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="688" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Number of High Confidence (HC) and Low Confidence (LC) gene models present in theÂ <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly. The total number of predicted genes was 56,469. LC1 plus HC is approximately the number of genes expected for the genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">
                           <i>Eragrostis curvula</i> Genome Evolution Among the Poaceae Family</h3><p>The duplication events of the Victoria cv. genome over the time has been evaluated through a paralogous genes analysis (Supplementary FileÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM2">1</a>). The Ks (synonymous substitutions rate) peaks show two paleopoliploidy events across the time, the ancestral paleoduplication event shared by all the grasses estimated that have occurred 80â90âMya (Millions the years ago) and a recent (4â5âMya) duplication before the <i>in vitro</i> culture diploidization of Victoria cv. (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig5">5</a>). Since the ancestral whole genome duplication a high contraction rate over the time was observed. In the BEP (Bambusoideae, Ehrhartoideae, and Pooideae) divergence from the PACCAD (Panicoideae, Arundinoideae, Chloridoideae, Centothecoideae, Aristidoideae, Danthonioideae) a 2.44 contraction/expansion rate was observed (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig6">6</a>). After that, the corresponding rate in the Panicoideae-Chloridoideae divergence was 6.2. Then, in the divergence between <i>Eragrostis</i> and <i>Oropetium</i> the rate increased up to 16.88. The contraction rate for <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i> genome assembly was 7.14 being the expansion higher than the contraction in all the evolution history after the grasses whole genome duplication (gWGD). More recently, during the <i>Eragrostis</i> speciation the rate was stabilized in 1.04 and after the <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> allotetraploidization (9â10âMya) the rate increased again to 2.97. Correspondingly, <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> presentÂ an allotetraploidization event 4â5âMya. However, it is not possible to calculate the gene loss after tetraplodization because one copy of the genome was lost during the <i>in vitro</i> diploidization event. The high proportion of expansions in <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> could be related to the low confidence genes models.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="732" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Distribution of the estimatedÂ synonymous substitutions rate (Ks) between <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and the selected Poaceae orthologous genes. Peaks represent the divergence between <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and the selected species. (<b>b</b>) Distribution of the synonymous substitution rate within each selected genome paralogous genes. The whole genome duplication shared by all the grasses (gWGD) was estimated to occur 90âMya. The peaks represent the tetraploidization events of <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> (tWGD) <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> (cWGD) and <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i> (mWGD).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="360" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree of selected Poaceae species based on 1,185 orthogroups. The time scale above the tree indicates the evolution time in millions of years ago (Mya). The numbers in red in the nodes show the number of contracted gene families while the numbers in green refer to the number of expansions. The ancient WGD (red star) was calculated to happen around ~90âMya. The <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i>, <i>E</i>. <i>tef and E</i>. <i>curvula</i> tetraploidization events (blue stars) were placed at 24.1, 9.6 and 4.5âMya, respectively. The yellow star represents the hexaploidization of <i>T</i>. <i>aestivum</i>. C4 grasses species are included in the red box and C3 in blue box.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The gene models from selected monocots species, such as <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> (A and B genome), <i>Triticum aestivum</i> (A, B and D genome), <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i>, <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i>, <i>S</i>. <i>italica</i>, <i>Panicum hallii</i>, <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i>, <i>B</i>. <i>distachyon</i> and <i>Musa itinerans</i> were grouped in orthogroups with the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genes models. The total number of defined orthogroups was 24,747, with 9,189 groups shared by the eleven species (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S5</a>). To assess the evolutionary relationships within the subfamily we grouped them into Chloridoideae, Panicoideae, Pooideae and Ehrhartoideae. The number of gene families shared by the Chloridoideae subfamily was 12,940, while the number of genes families that are in common within the Panicoideae and Pooideae subfamilies were 13,621 and 15,532, respectively (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S6</a>). Among the C4 grasses 11,570 common orthogroups were identified between both subfamilies, whereas 2,051 and 1,370 were found to be specific to the Panicoideae and the Chloridoideae subfamily, respectively. When an analogous analysis was performed with the C3 species, 14,203 shared orthogroups were identified, whereas 1,329 and 2,601 orthogroups were unique to the Pooideae and the Ehrhartoideae subfamily, respectively.</p><p>When the assembled genomes sharing orthogroups were analyzed, we found that the second most abundant group of species sharing orthogroups wasÂ constituted by a combination of two species (3,991 orthogroups). The combination of <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> and <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> contributed to this group with 616 orthogroups, representing 15.5% of the total (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S5</a>). This means that 616 orthogroups were shared by these two species exclusively being <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> the most closely related species to <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i>. This was confirmed by the construction of a phylogenetic tree with the eleven species, in which the divergence between <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> was calculated as occurring 14.5âMya and, as was expected, <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> was located close to the other C4 grasses (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig6">6</a>).</p><p>Using SyMAP to plot the syntenic regions between <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and the other monocots species it was found that 79% of the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly length is covered by the <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i> and <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i> syntenic blocks (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig7">7</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S7</a>). These analyses also revealed the presence of 182 reverse blocks between <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i> and 149 between <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i>, representing genome rearrangements that occurred during the evolution of these grasses. Despite the divergence, 98% of the <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> assembly was covered by <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> scaffolds, sharing 262 syntenic blocks. Interestingly, <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> chromosome 3 is fully covered in the same orientation by theÂ <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> Contig 3 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig7">7</a>), indicating the close conservation of this chromosome among these grasses. The syntenic analysis over the <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i> genome assembly revealed that 96% of the genome was covered by the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> scaffolds (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S7</a>). However, different patterns were observed between the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> scaffolds and the <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i> chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig8">8</a>). For example, <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> Contig 3, that completely covers <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> chromosome 3, also covers the entire <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i> chromosome 4, whereas <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i> chromosome 3 is completely covered by <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> Contigs 25 and 8, suggesting that these two <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> scaffolds constitute one single chromosome. Other <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i> chromosomes, such as 1, 2, 4, 5, 7, 8 and 10, are fully covered by Contigs 10, 38, 28, 6, 12 and 1, respectively, even when several rearrangements were detected.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Figure 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="602" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Synteny between <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and: (<b>a</b>) <i>Zea mays;</i> (<b>b</b>) <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor;</i> (<b>c</b>) <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i> and (<b>d</b>) <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> genome assemblies. In <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> the fourteen longest scaffolds are represented. Seventy nine percent ofÂ the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome was covered by the <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i> and <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i>, 85% by <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i> and 84% byÂ <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> genomes,Â respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button"
