Exceptional subgenome stability and functional divergence in allotetraploid teff, the primary cereal crop in Ethiopia. bioRxiv 580720, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/580720&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d60274e1996">26</a></sup> ratifies the chromosome scale of the Contigs 10, 12, 38, 6, 3, 1, 28 and provides evidences that the pairs of Contigs 8â25, 50â4, and 30â44 correspond to the <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> chromosomes 3, 8 and 7 respectively, thus, assuming that there are not changes in the chromosome structure the ten <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> chromosomes seem to be present in the assembly with a high level of contiguity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genetic Relationships Among <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> Genotypes Assessed by SSR Analyses</h3><p>SSR specific primers previously designed from transcriptomic sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Garbus, I. et al. De novo transcriptome sequencing and assembly from apomictic and sexual Eragrostis curvula genotypes. PloS one. 12, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0185595&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2029">9</a></sup> were mapped onto the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly. Twenty eight out of the 35 reported <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> SSRs primers (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S8</a>) gave 100% identity and 100% of coverage with the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly. Additional SSR primers were designed based on the cv. Victoria assembly. Regarding the newly designed primers over the Victoria genome 14 out of 15 amplified with the expected amplicons size (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S7</a>).</p><p>A phylogenetic tree was constructed using the Jaccard distance matrix calculated from the SSR markers (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig9">9</a>). This tree grouped the heptaploid cultivars Don Luis and Don Pablo together, showing the similarity between each other and the divergence from the other cultivars. Cultivar Victoria was located close to the diploid PI299920 and in the same branch as the tetraploid cultivars Tanganyika INTA and Tanganyika USDA. Tanganyika INTA is an apomictic tetraploid cultivar that gave rise to cv. Victoria through chromosome reduction by <i>in vitro</i> inflorescence culture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cardone, S. et al. Novel genotypes of the subtropical grass Eragrostis curvula for the study of apomixis (diplospory). Euphytica. 151, 263â272 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2067">27</a></sup>. Due to its similarity with cv. Victoria, it is a candidate for sequencing to identify the genomic region controlling apomixis.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Figure 9"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 9</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig9_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig9_HTML.png" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" width="685" height="614" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>Phylogenetic tree of different cultivars of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> constructed using the Jaccard distance matrix calculated from SSR markers. Cultivar Victoria was placed next to the diploid cultivar PI299920 and the tetraploid Tanganyika INTA.Â The diploidization of this cultivar through <i>in vitro</i> inflorescence culture gave rise to the Victoria cv.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Lignin Pathway</h3><p>Several reports have established that digestibility in forage grasses can be improved through downregulation of genes involved in the lignin pathway by genetic engineering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, L. et al. Improved forage digestibility of tall fescue (Festuca arundinacea) by transgenic downâregulation of cinnamyl alcohol dehydrogenase. Plant Biotechnol J. 1, 437â49 (2003)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2106">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="He, X., Hall, M. B., Gallo-Meagher, M. &amp; Smith, R. L. Improvement of forage quality by downregulation of maize O-methyltransferase. Crop Sci. 43, 2240â2251 (2003)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2106_1">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Giordano, A. et al. Reduced lignin content and altered lignin composition in the warm season forage grass Paspalum dilatatum by down-regulation of a Cinnamoyl CoA reductase gene. Transgenic Res. 23, 503â517 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2109">30</a></sup>. Therefore, sequence information of lignin biosynthetic genes and their controlling elements is crucial to manipulate their expression. Using the KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Kanehisa, M., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M., Morishima, K. &amp; Tanabe, M. New approach for understanding genome variations in KEGG. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, 590â595 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2113">31</a></sup> database 16 gene models (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S8</a>) were found to be involved in the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> lignin biosynthesis pathway, from the first gene, corresponding to phenylalanine ammonia-lyase, to the final products, guaiacyl (G), p-hydroxyphenyl (H) and syringyl lignin (S) monolignols.</p><p>From this pathway we focused on the enzyme caffeoyl shikimate esterase (CSE) because of its recently identified role in lignin biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Vanholme, R. et al. Caffeoyl shikimate esterase (CSE) is an enzyme in the lignin biosynthetic pathway in Arabidopsis. Science. 341, 1103â1106 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2129">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ha, C. M. et al. An essential role of caffeoyl shikimate esterase in monolignol biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula. Plant J. 86, 363â375 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2132">33</a></sup>. This enzyme affects the production of caffeoyl-coenzyme A 3-O-methyltransferase, previously studied by our group in <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="DÃ­az, M. L., Garbus, I. &amp; Echenique, V. Allele-specific expression of a weeping lovegrass gene from the lignin biosynthetic pathway, caffeoyl-coenzyme A 3-O-methyltransferase. Mol Breed. 64, 627â637 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2141">34</a></sup> which could be used together with CSE to improve forage quality. Genes encoding class II enzymes are widespread in the plant kingdom, while genes encoding class I CSE enzymes are not present in all species. Aligning class I CSE from <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> and class II from <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i> against the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly we could identify both geneÂ CSE classes. CSE genes were amplified from <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> using specifically designed PCR primers (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S9</a>), and the resulting amplicons were cloned and sequenced, obtaining a perfect match with the sequences from the genome assembly. Moreover, our previous transcriptomic analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Garbus, I. et al. De novo transcriptome sequencing and assembly from apomictic and sexual Eragrostis curvula genotypes. PloS one. 12, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0185595&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2174">9</a></sup> showed the presence of mRNA from both genes, indicating that they are actively transcribed in this species.</p><p>The sequences of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> class I and II genes were aligned to the corresponding sequences from other members of the Poaceae family (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S10</a>). The two classes were found in <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>hallii</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> and only class II was present in <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i>, <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i>, <i>S</i>. <i>italic</i>, <i>B</i>. <i>distachyon</i> and <i>T</i>. <i>aestivum</i>. We found single equal-sized amplicons in <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> and <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> for class I CSE (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S9</a>). Cloning these amplicons confirmed the existence of CSE class I in both species (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S10</a>). Looking into the evolution of the grasses (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig6">6</a>) it is possible to deduce that the loss of the CSE class I occurred at different times during the divergence of these species, since the enzyme is absent from the Pooideae subfamily, present in the Chloridoideae and appears only in some members of the Panicoideae.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">WRKY Transcription Factor Family</h3><p>The WRKY transcription factor family is one of the most studied gene families associated with biotic and abiotic stresses in plants. Since <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> is a species adapted to high temperature, high radiation and drought stress, classification of its WRKY transcription factors is central to understand the mechanism(s) involved in its tolerance to these conditions.</p><p>From the Pfam annotated gene models, 74 genes with WRKY and zinc finger motifs were found (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S11</a>). Seven out of the 74 were classified as group I, 32 as group II and 35 as group III. Group II was divided into five subclasses according to the remaining sequence motifs and the phylogenetic distribution (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#Fig10">10</a>). The EcWRKY family has 39 of its members clustered into 13 genomic regions of less than 100âkb while other members are isolated (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S11</a>). This spatial distribution agrees with previously reported data for other grass species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Muthamilarasan, M. et al. Global analysis of WRKY transcription factor superfamily in Setaria identifies potential candidates involved in abiotic stress signaling. Front Plant Sci. 26, 910, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2015.00910&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2015)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2289">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wei, K. F., Chen, J., Chen, Y. F., Wu, L. J. &amp; Xie, D. X. Molecular phylogenetic and expression analysis of the complete WRKY transcription factor family in maize. DNA res. 19, 153â64 (2012)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2289_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ning, P., Liu, C., Kang, J. &amp; Lv, J. Genome-wide analysis of WRKY transcription factors in wheat (Triticum aestivum L.) and differential expression under water deficit condition. PeerJ. 5, 3232, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.7717/peerj.3232&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2292">37</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-10" data-title="Figure 10"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig10" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 10</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/10" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig10_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-10-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-46610-0/MediaObjects/41598_2019_46610_Fig10_HTML.png" alt="figure10" loading="lazy" width="685" height="947" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-10-desc"><p>Phylogenetic tree and sequence motifs of the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> WRKY transcription factor family. Bars with the same shape and color represent the same motif. Seven proteins were classified as group I (red), 32 as group II (green) and 35 as group III (blue).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0/figures/10" data-track-dest="link:Figure10 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Here we present the first high-quality genome assembly of a diploid genotypeÂ of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i>. This diploid assembly is a starting point for the genome assembly of the most complex polyploids of the same genus, which harbor the region(s) that controls diplosporous apomixis, and may allow us to assess the complex relationship between apomixis and ploidy.</p><p>The final FALCON assembly, after polishing, rendered an N50 of 0.380âMb, 3,118 contigs and 96% of complete BUSCO genes. At this point, due to the size, the complexity and repetitiveness of the <i>E. curvula</i> genome, we could not achieve the assembly metrics reached by other plant genomes based on PacBio assembly alone. However, the N50 of our assembly was higher than the ones obtained for other grasses using other sequencing technologies, as was the case for <i>Aegilops tauschii</i> (4.3âGb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature. 551, 498â502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2344">38</a></sup>, <i>Triticum urartu</i> (4.94âGb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ling, H. Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature. 496, 87â90 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2351">39</a></sup> and <i>S</i>. <i>Italica</i> (490âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2362">23</a></sup> with N50 values of 0.207âMb, 0.064âMb and 0.254âMb, respectively.</p><p>The promising results obtained with the FALCON assembly encouraged us to look for a scaffolding technology to increase genome assembly contiguity. One of the latest methodologies to obtain chromosome-scale scaffolds is the proximity ligation-based technology used by Chicago and Hi-C. The combination of these technologies with the FALCON assembly increased the N50 to 43.41âMb and decreased the number of scaffolds to 1,143. The only currently available report about this combination of technologies is for <i>D</i>. <i>zibethinus</i> (738âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Teh, B. T. et al. The draft genome of tropical fruit durian (Durio zibethinus). Nat genet. 46, 1633â1641 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2375">20</a></sup>, in which an N50 of 22.7âMb was achieved, half of the final <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> N50. Other assemblies using a different combination of technologies such as optical mapping and Hi-C, for <i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> (1.45âGb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jarvis, D. E. et al. The genome of Chenopodium quinoa. Nature. 542, 307â301 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2389">16</a></sup>, <i>M</i>. <i>truncatula</i> (465âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Moll, K. M. et al. Strategies for optimizing BioNano and Dovetail explored through a second reference quality assembly for the legume model, Medicago truncatula. BMC genomics. 18, 578, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/s12864-017-3971-4&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2399">40</a></sup> and <i>Arabis alpine</i> (370âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Jiao, W. B. et al. Improving and correcting the contiguity of long-read genome assemblies of three plant species using optical mapping and chromosome conformation capture data. Genome Res. 27, 778â786 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2407">41</a></sup>, rendered N50 values of 3.84âMb, 12.5âMb and 31âMb, respectively. A combination of optical mapping and Chicago led to an N50 of 95âkb in <i>Hevea brasiliensis</i> (2.15âGb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Pootakham, W. et al. De novo hybrid assembly of the rubber tree genome reveals evidence of paleotetraploidy in Hevea species. Sci Rep. 7, 41457, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1038/srep41457&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2414">42</a></sup>. In <i>Manihot esculenta</i> (1.23âGb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Bredeson, J. V. et al. Sequencing wild and cultivated cassava and related species reveals extensive interspecific hybridization and genetic diversity. Nat Biotechnol. 34, 562â270 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2421">43</a></sup> Chicago only was used with a resulting N50 value of 27.7âkb. These results show that the combination of Hi-C and Chicago is very powerful, increasing the N50 more than other combinations of technologies.</p><p>Our final <i>E. curvula</i> genome assembly has 96.4% of complete BUSCO genes, covers 97% of the estimated genome length and contains 95.5% of theÂ DArT markers, all this components suggest the near completeness of this assembly.</p><p>An important feature of grass genomes is the presence of repetitive elements that differ in number and complexity among species, being higher in more complex genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Dubin, M. J., Scheid, O. M. &amp; Becker, C. Transposons: a blessing curse. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 42, 23â29 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2435">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Lee, S. I. &amp; Kim, N. S. Transposable elements and genome size variations in plants. Genomics inform. 12, 87â97 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2438">45</a></sup>. In the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly we found 28.8% of repetitive elements, a similar value to the 27.46% reported for <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Gebre, Y. G., Bertolini, E., PÃ¨, M. E. &amp; Zuccolo, A. Identification and characterization of abundant repetitive sequences in Eragrostis tef cv. Enatite genome. BMC Plant Biol. 16, 39, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/s12870-016-0725-4&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2453">22</a></sup> and 21.4% for <i>B</i>. <i>distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Vogel, J. P. et al. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature. 463, 763â768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2463">46</a></sup>, but lower than the 46.44% found in <i>S</i>. <i>italica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2472">23</a></sup> and the 62% from <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2482">24</a></sup>.</p><p>The number of gene models found in <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> was 56,469. The BUSCO analysis performed on these models resulted in 28.1% of duplicated BUSCO genes. This overestimation seems to occur in most species, for example, the recently published <i>T</i>. <i>aestivum</i> genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Appels, R. et al. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science. 361, 6403, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1126/science.aar7191&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2501">47</a></sup> has 259,979 gene models, a very high number of genes even for an hexaploid species. From the gene models, we assessed the evolution of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> within the Poaceae family, positioning the species close to <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> and finding 9,189 orthogroups shared by the selected Poaceae subfamilies.</p><p>Seventy-four WRKY transcription factors were identified in the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly, and classified into three main groups and five subgroups, based on the results obtained by other researchers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Eulgem, T. &amp; Somssich, I. E. Networks of WRKY transcription factors in defense signaling. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 10, 366â371 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2527">48</a></sup>. The identification, classification and characterization of this gene family constitute a key step into the elucidation of the molecular basis of the drought tolerance of this species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Colom, M. R. &amp; Vazzana, C. Water stress effects on three cultivars of Eragrostis curvula. Ital. j. agron. 6, 127â32 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2531">49</a></sup> since it allows the design of further specific expression studies that would contribute to dissect their involvement in this important trait of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i>.</p><p>One of the main limiting factors of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> as a forage source it is its low digestibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Gargano, A. O., AdÃºriz, M. A., Arelovich, H. M. &amp; Amela, M. I. Forage yield and nutritive value of Eragrostis curvula and Digitaria eriantha in central-south semi-arid Argentina. Trop. grassl. 35, 161â167 (2001)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2551">50</a></sup> a trait that has the potential to be improved by transgenic modification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Giordano, A. et al. Reduced lignin content and altered lignin composition in the warm season forage grass Paspalum dilatatum by down-regulation of a Cinnamoyl CoA reductase gene. Transgenic Res. 23, 503â517 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2555">30</a></sup>. Two classes of CSE, an enzyme involved in lignin reduction, have been described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Vanholme, R. et al. Caffeoyl shikimate esterase (CSE) is an enzyme in the lignin biosynthetic pathway in Arabidopsis. Science. 341, 1103â1106 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2559">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ha, C. M. et al. An essential role of caffeoyl shikimate esterase in monolignol biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula. Plant J. 86, 363â375 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2562">33</a></sup>, with class II present in all species examined, whereas class I is only present in someÂ of them. Interestingly, class II was present in all the evaluated Chloridoideae species, such as <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i>, <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i> and <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i>, finding not previously reported (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S10</a>).</p><p>In conclusion, using a combination of different technologies to assemble and validate the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome a notable advance in the <i>de novo</i> assembly of non-model genomes was achieved. This assembled genome also provides an invaluable tool to find new targets for crop improvement regarding classical focused traits, such as drought tolerance and digestibility. Finally, the availability of this assembly provides the foundation for the assembly of more complex tetraploid apomictic <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genomes, aiding in the study of the reproductive mode.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant Material</h3><p>DNA for genome sequencing was extracted from <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> cv. Victoria, a sexual diploid (2nâ=â2xâ=â20) genotype obtained from <i>in vitro</i> culture of inflorescences of cv. Tanganyika<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cardone, S. et al. Novel genotypes of the subtropical grass Eragrostis curvula for the study of apomixis (diplospory). Euphytica. 151, 263â272 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2628">27</a></sup> and registered at the National Cultivar Register, Argentina (UNST1122, RC9192). This accession couldnât be selfed to decrease the heterozygosity because its self-incompatibility. Leaf samples for DNA extraction were collected from a plant growing in the greenhouse at CERZOS, CCT â CONICET BahÃ­a Blanca, Argentina. DNA for DArT markers was extracted from leaves of a mapping population consisting of 63 individuals derived from the cross between two tetraploid <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> cultivars, OTA x Don Walter, and from two samples of cv. Victoria. DNA for SSRs amplification was extracted from leaves of different <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> cultivars: USDA accessions PI208214 (2x), PI299919 (2x), PI299920 (2x), PI299928 (2x), PI574506 (OTA, 4x), PI234217 (Tanganyika, 4x), and cultivars from INTA germplasm collection Tanganyika (4x), Don Walter (4x), Don Pablo (7x) and Don Luis (7x) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">DNA Extraction</h3><p>DNA samples for PacBio long reads and DArT sequencing were obtained from 80âmg of fresh leaf tissue using a CTAB-based method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Meier, M., Zappacosta, D., Selva, J. P., Pessino, S. &amp; Echenique, V. Evaluation of different methods for assessing the reproductive mode of weeping lovegrass plants, Eragrostis curvula (Schrad.) Nees. Aust J Bot. 59, 253â61 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2656">51</a></sup>. The protocol was adapted to obtain large DNA molecules (gDNA), taking care in the critical steps to avoid breaking, resulting in an average length of 41,800âbp and a concentration of 0.7880âng/Î¼L.</p><p>For the Chicago and Dovetail Hi-C proximity ligation libraries, fresh leaf tissue from the same plant was delivered to Dovetail Genomics (<a href="http://www.dovetailgenomics.com">www.dovetailgenomics.com</a>) for extraction and sequencing of pure DNA and endogenous chromatin using a proximity ligation-optimized Dovetail in-house protocol.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Library Preparation and Sequencing</h3><p>Long-read sequencing of cv. Victoria DNA was performed using Pacific Bioscienceâs Single Molecule Real-Time (SMRT) chemistry through the Sequel platform (<a href="http://www.pacb.com">www.pacb.com</a>) at the University of Liverpool Centre for Genomic Research (UK). Current PacBio systems generate reads with an average size of nearly 20âkb and a maximum length of over 60âkb. Two LibrariesÂ of 10âkb and 20âkbÂ were Â prepared through the BluePippin (Sage Science) fragment selection method (<a href="http://www.sagescience.com/products/bluepippin/">http://www.sagescience.com/products/bluepippin/</a>). After repairing the ends and an adapter ligation process the libraries were sequenced. The coverage of the estimated haplotype (620âMb) was 90X, 10X greater than the one recommended for this technology for a genome with characteristics such as that of <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> (<a href="http://www.pacb.com/calculator-whole-genome-sequencing/">www.pacb.com/calculator-whole-genome-sequencing/</a>).</p><p>To improve the assembly contiguity and to orientate the contigs, Chicago and Dovetail Hi-C libraries were sequenced starting from endogenous chromatin and high molecular weight DNA, respectively. The Chicago and Dovetail Hi-C libraries were sequenced through a 2âÃâ150 paired-end Illumina HiSeq2500 platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome Assembly</h3><p>The PacBio raw reads were assembled with FALCON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat Methods. 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2716">52</a></sup> and Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2720">53</a></sup> software exploring different parameters (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S13</a>). The assembly quality was assessed by comparing numerous metrics (N50, assembly size, number of contigs and average contig length). The assembly was also evaluated using BUSCO v.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2727">54</a></sup>. This software uses a large selection of widespread orthologous single-copy genes as benchmarks to gauge theÂ completeness of the novel assembled genome. The assembly with the highest N50, the least number of contigs, the highest average contig length and with more complete, less fragmented and/or missing BUSCO genes, was chosen.</p><p>After this procedure, the PacBio SMRT tool reference guide (<a href="https://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/SMRT-Tools-Reference-Guide-v4.0.0.pdf">https://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/SMRT-Tools-Reference-Guide-v4.0.0.pdf</a>) was followed to polish the draft genome assembly. The raw reads were aligned with the pbalign software against the assembly with the following criteria: minimum alignment length 50âbp; minimum similarity and minimum accuracy, 70%. The pbalign output was used as input for the Arrow software with the default parameters to polish the assembly by choosing the base with the highest coverage in each position.</p><p>The final assembly was obtained by scaffolding the polished genome assembly with the data obtained through the Chicago and Dovetail Hi-C libraries. The Dovetail Hi-Rise scaffolding software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Koch, L. Technique: Chicago HighRise for genome scaffolding. Nat Rev Genet. 17, 194, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1038/nrg.2016.23&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2744">55</a></sup> was used to integrate the data obtained from the Chicago library with the PacBio polished assembly. Finally, the Chicago assembly was combined with the Dovetail Hi-C files through Dovetail Hi-Rise to obtain scaffolds in ranges up to the size of whole chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Diversity Arrays Technology (DArT) Validation</h3><p>The entire DArT preparation procedure, including SNP calling, was provided by the Genetic Analysis Service for Agriculture Laboratory (SAGA, CIMMyT, MÃ©xico) using an in-house protocol. DArT technology uses the combination of <i>Pst</i>I and <i>Mse</i>I restriction enzymes in order to reduce the genome complexity. The process separate low copy sequences from the repetitive fraction of the genome (<a href="https://www.diversityarrays.com">https://www.diversityarrays.com</a>). The fragments obtained from the enzyme digestion were sequenced using the Illumina platform and the reads were used as input by the service provider protocol to <i>de novo</i> obtain the markers of 69âbp containing the SNP. The reads and markers are available under the NCBI bioproject PRJNA508722 (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/</a>) with the SNP position and polymorphism present in the header of each marker sequence. The SNP markers were mapped onto the whole genome assembly usingÂ the Bowtie software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Methods. 9, 357â359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2781">56</a></sup> with the end-to-end and -k 10 (up to 10 distinct valid alignments for each read) parameter to validate the final assembly with data from another source. Since the DArT libraries are designed to target active regions of the genome the 492,378 DArT reads were mapped with Bowtie onto all the scaffolds using the âk 10 and end-to-end parameter and the hits were plotted with a 500âkb window size.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Repetitive Sequence Assessment</h3><p>Repetitive sequences were assessed through three different approaches. The first one was based on the generation of a <i>de novo</i> library by the RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Smit, A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. Open-1.0, &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2008â2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2796">57</a></sup> software, that uses a modeling package to <i>de novo</i> identify repeat families. The second approach uses the TransposonPSI software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Altschul, S. F. &amp; Koonin, E. V. Iterated profile searches with PSI-BLASTâa tool for discovery in protein databases. Trends Biochem Sci. 23, 444â447 (1998)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2803">58</a></sup> that identifies repetitive elements based on the homology to protein or nucleic acid sequences to proteins encoded by diverse families of transposable elements. The result of each program is merged with USERCH v7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Edgar, R. C. Search and clustering orders of magnitude faster than BLAST. Bioinformatics. 26, 2460â2461 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2807">59</a></sup> taking only a single record when the repetition is included in both programs. The merged file is then classified with the RepeatClassifier script (included in the RepeatModeler package) according to the structure and the type of element present in the sequence in one of the main classes of repetitions (ALU, LINE, LTR, DNA elements and Unknown elements). The sequences were classified to subclass and superfamily level depending if they were complete or not. The final approach consisted of finding homologous repetitive elements in related monocot species. RepBase23, a reference database of repetitive DNA sequences from different eukaryotic species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mobile DNA. 6, 11, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkp335&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2812">60</a></sup>, was used to find homologous sequences. Here, consensus sequences of large families and subfamilies of repeats from <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i>, <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i> were used.</p><p>The previously reported ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Romero, J., Selva, J. P., Pessino, S., Echenique, V. &amp; Garbus, I. Repetitive sequences in Eragrostis curvula cDNA EST libraries obtained from genotypes with different ploidy. Biol Plant. 60, 55â67 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2834">25</a></sup> related to repetitive elements were aligned to the repetitive elements present in the assembled <i>E. curvula</i> genome in order to find the complete structureÂ of these elements using the BLASTn algorithm with an e-value of 1.0âe<sup>â10</sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene Annotation</h3><p>In order to annotate the gene models present in the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genome assembly, the repetitive DNA was masked using the RepeatMasker software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Smit, A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0, &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013â2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2857">61</a></sup> using the <i>de novo</i> and the homology-based fasta files with the library (âlib) parameter. The -s parameter was used to increase sensitivity in the masking process. To find homology, RMblast, a RepeatMasker-compatible version of the standard NCBI BLASTn program, was used. The main difference between these two programs is that RMblast is optimized to compare the RepeatMasker matrix.</p><p>After this procedure two different approaches were used to annotate the genes; the first consisted of the alignment of the genome scaffolds against the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> floral transcriptome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Garbus, I. et al. De novo transcriptome sequencing and assembly from apomictic and sexual Eragrostis curvula genotypes. PloS one. 12, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0185595&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2873">9</a></sup> and ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Cervigni, G. D. et al. Expressed sequence tag analysis and development of gene associated markers in a near-isogenic plant system of Eragrostis curvula. Plant Mol Biol. 67, 1â10 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2877">62</a></sup>, and protein data from related species like <i>Eragrostis tef</i>â<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cannarozzi, G. et al. Genome and transcriptome sequencing identifies breeding targets in the orphan crop tef (Eragrostis tef). BMC genomics. 15, 581, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2164-15-581&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2885">8</a></sup>, <i>S</i>. <i>italica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2894">23</a></sup>, <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2903">24</a></sup> and <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature. 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2913">17</a></sup>. This analysis was performed using the Exonerate software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC bioinformatics. 6, 31, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-6-31&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2917">63</a></sup>, a general tool for sequence comparisons, setting the minimum alignment coverage to 80% and the minimum identity to 85%. The second method included <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction, an intrinsic method based on gene content and signal detection in which the genomic DNA sequence alone is systematically searched for certain tell-tale signs of protein-coding genes. For this, we used AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 309â312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2924">64</a></sup> an HMM-based (Hidden Markov Model) gene finder and SNAP software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Johnson, A. D. et al. SNAP: a web-based tool for identification and annotation of proxy SNPs using HapMap. Bioinformatics. 24, 2938â2939 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2929">65</a></sup>. In <i>ab initio</i> prediction is necessary to train the programs in order to create the best model to precisely find the genes. For this purpose, the model used to find the complete genes was extracted from the output of the BUSCO software.</p><p>The annotation was performed through the MAKER software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Campbell, M. S., Holt, C., Moore, B. &amp; Yandell, M. Genome annotation and curation using MAKER and MAKERâP. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics 48, 11â39 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2939">66</a></sup>, using as input the RepeatMasker output and the protein and RNA alignment obtained from Exonerate in a splice-aware fashion to accurately identify splicing sites. MAKER also uses the gene models predicted by AUGUSTUS and SNAP, compares all the predicted gene models to RNA and protein alignment evidence, and then revises the <i>ab initio</i> gene models in order to predict the most confident gene models.</p><p>To assess the annotation completeness, the classification strategy adopted for <i>T</i>. <i>aestivum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Appels, R. et al. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science. 361, 6403, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1126/science.aar7191&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2954">47</a></sup> was followed. This strategy classified the genes in three main categories, high confidence (HC), low confidence (LC), and transposons (TREP), based on the completeness (start and stop codon) and on the homology (coverageââ¥â90%; e-valueââ¤â10âe<sup>â10</sup>) to unipoa (Poaceae proteins, SwissProt and trEMBL), unimag (Magnoliophyta proteins, SwissProt) and TREP database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Schulman, A. H. &amp; Wicker, T. A Field Guide to Transposable Elements. In Plant Transposons and Genome Dynamics in Evolution (ed. Fedoroff, N. V.) 15â40 (John Wiley &amp; Sons, Pennsylvania State, University, 2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2960">67</a></sup> (transposons database) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S14</a>).</p><p>Finally, InterProScan version 5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Finn, R. D. et al. InterPro in 2017âbeyond protein family and domain annotations. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, 190â199 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2971">68</a></sup> was used to classify genes into families and predict the presence of domains and important sites. This software uses 14 different databases, retrieving information such as KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Kanehisa, M., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M., Morishima, K. &amp; Tanabe, M. New approach for understanding genome variations in KEGG. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, 590â595 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2975">31</a></sup> pathways, gene ontology and Pfam domains.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Synteny Analysis</h3><p>SyMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Soderlund, C., Bomhoff, M. &amp; Nelson, W. SyMAP: A turnkey synteny system with application to plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 39(10), e68, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkr123&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d60274e2987">69</a></sup> software was used to search for homologies among <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and the genomic regions of other grasses. This tool generates whole genome synteny patterns plots between two organisms. The selected species for these comparisons were other C4 grasses, such as <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i>â<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Exceptional subgenome stability and functional divergence in allotetraploid teff, the primary cereal crop in Ethiopia. bioRxiv 580720, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/580720&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3004">26</a></sup> <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3013">24</a></sup>, <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature. 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3022">17</a></sup>, <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Kawahara, Y. et al. Improvement of the Oryza sativa Nipponbare reference genome using next generation sequence and optical map data. Rice. 6, 4, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/1939-8433-6-4&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3032">70</a></sup> and <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature. 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3041">14</a></sup>. SyMAP uses the alignment tool BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3045">71</a></sup>, run with the following parameters: -minScoreâ=â30, -minIdentityâ=â70, -tileSizeâ=â10, -qMaskâ=âlower, and -maxIntronâ=â10000.</p><p>The paralogous and orthologous syntenic genes obtained were listed (Supplementary FilesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM2">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM3">2</a>) and the Ks substitution rate was calculated for each pair of genes using the BioPerl package through the NeiâGojobori method. To find WGD events the Ks rate was plotted and the peaks were corrected using the method proposed by Wang <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Wang, X. et al. Genome alignment spanning major Poaceae lineages reveals heterogeneous evolutionary rates and alters inferred dates for key evolutionary events. Mol Plant. 8, 885â98 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3061">72</a></sup>. Results were visualized in a circle plot showing the shared genomic regions with different colors and a table was constructed showing the number of anchors and blocks common to <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i>, <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i>, <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i>, <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i>, <i>O sativa</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Analysis of the Evolutionary Relationships Among the Poaceae Family</h3><p>The evolutionary relationships among <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> and monocots species like <i>S</i>. <i>italica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, G. et al. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science. 361, 6403, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1126/science.aar7191&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3129">47</a></sup>, <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i>â<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cannarozzi, G. et al. BMC genomics. 15, 581, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2164-15-581&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3139">8</a></sup>, <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Finn, R. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, 190â199 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3149">68</a></sup>, <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Nature. 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3158">17</a></sup>, <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3168">24</a></sup>, <i>B</i>. <i>distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Vogel, J. P. et al. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature. 463, 763â768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3177">46</a></sup>, <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Nature. 527, 508â511 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3186">14</a></sup>, <i>Musa itinerans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Wu, W. et al. Sci Rep 6, 31586 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3193">73</a></sup> and <i>P</i>. <i>hallii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Lovell, J. T. et al. The genomic landscape of molecular responses to natural drought stress in Panicum hallii. Nature com. 9, 5213 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3202">74</a></sup>, were assessed by comparing the assembled genome annotations using the software Orthofinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1186/s13059-015-0721-2&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3206">75</a></sup>. This software groups in orthogroups genes originating from the same common ancestor and creates trees for each group and for all the species. The alignment of the orthogroups for all the species was used to create a maximum likelihood time phylogenetic tree using the Jones Taylor Thornton model with the software MEGAX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Li, M., Knyaz, C. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA X: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis across Computing Platforms. Mol Biol Evol. 35, 1547â1549 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3210">76</a></sup>. The calibration was performed according to Prasad <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Prasad, V., StrÃ¶mberg, C. A., Alimohammadian, H. &amp; Sahni, A. Dinosaur coprolites and the early evolution of grasses and grazers. Science 310, 1177â1180 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3218">77</a></sup> considering the divergence time between <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> and <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i> in approximately 70âMya. <i>M</i>. <i>itinierans</i> was used as outgroup.</p><p>Gene expansion and contraction were assessed by the CAFEv3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol Biol Evol. 30, 1987â1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3244">78</a></sup> software using the genes families obtained from Orthofinder. Multiple birth-death lambda (Î») was used in order to assess the different clade evolution rates.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Classification of the WRKY Transcription Factor Family</h3><p>To identify the WRKYs from <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> the annotated genes with WRKY motifs were extracted from Pfam. The genes were filtered with the MEME software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Bailey, T. L. et al. MEME SUITE: tools for motif discovery and searching. Nuc acids research. 37, 202â208 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3262">79</a></sup>, and those genes showing complete WRKY and zinc finger motifs were classified into three main groups (I, II and III) and into five subgroups (IIa, IIb, IIc, IId and IIe)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Eulgem, T. &amp; Somssich, I. E. Networks of WRKY transcription factors in defense signaling. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 10, 366â371 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3266">48</a></sup>. Those genes with two WRKY motifs were classified in group I and those with only one into groups II and III. Then, if the terminal region of the zinc finger was HâX<sub>1</sub>âH, the gene was classified as group II and if the terminal region was HâX<sub>1</sub>âC, the gene was classified as group III. Based on the remaining motif of the sequences, group II was classified into five subgroups. Finally, to find the differences among the groups, a multiple sequence alignment was run with the MUSCLE software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3275">80</a></sup>, all the groups and subgroups were plotted in a phylogenetic tree constructed using MEGAX software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Li, M., Knyaz, C. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA X: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis across Computing Platforms. Mol Biol Evol. 35, 1547â1549 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3279">76</a></sup> with a maximum likelihood model</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Analysis of Genes Involved in the Lignin Pathway</h3><p>The KASS online tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Moriya, Y., Itoh, M., Okuda, S., Yoshizawa, A. C. &amp; Kanehisa, M. KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 182â185 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3292">81</a></sup> assigns a biological role to new genes using the Ghostx aligner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Suzuki, S., Kakuta, M., Ishida, T. &amp; Akiyama, Y. GHOSTX: an improved sequence homology search algorithm using a query suffix array and a database suffix array. PloS one. 9, e103833, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0103833&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3296">82</a></sup>, finding homology to known sequences in the KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Kanehisa, M., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M., Morishima, K. &amp; Tanabe, M. New approach for understanding genome variations in KEGG. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, 590â595 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3300">31</a></sup> database and assigning them a position in a pathway. Genes for class I and class II caffeoyl shikimate esterases (CSE) were targeted, since both have been recently mentioned as having important roles in the regulation of the lignin pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Vanholme, R. et al. Caffeoyl shikimate esterase (CSE) is an enzyme in the lignin biosynthetic pathway in Arabidopsis. Science. 341, 1103â1106 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3304">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ha, C. M. et al. An essential role of caffeoyl shikimate esterase in monolignol biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula. Plant J. 86, 363â375 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3307">33</a></sup>. For BLASTn, a class I <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i> orthologous gene (accession XM_015768109.2) and a class II <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i> orthologous gene (accession XM_002462989.2) were used as queries. Specific primers for PCR amplification of these genes were designed based on the genome sequence, the PCR program consisted of an initial DNA denaturation at 94âÂ°C for 2âmin, followed by 38 cycles at 94âÂ°C for 15âs, 50âÂ°C for 20âs and 72âÂ°C for 80âs and a final extension of 5âmin at 72âÂ°C. Amplicons were analyzed over 1.5% agarose gel. The amplicons were cloned into the pGEM-T Easy Vector (Promega), sequenced and BLAST searched were performed against the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> transcripts, and the <i>P halli</i>, <i>O</i>. <i>sativa</i>, <i>E</i>. <i>tef</i>, <i>O</i>. <i>thomaeum</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, <i>T</i>. <i>aestivum</i>, <i>Z</i>. <i>mays</i>, <i>S</i>. <i>bicolor</i> and <i>S italica</i> genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">SSRs Discovery and Analysis of Genetic Relationships within the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> Complex</h3><p>SSRs discovery was conducted through SSR Locator software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Da Maia, L. C. et al. SSR locator: tool for simple sequence repeat discovery integrated with primer design and PCR simulation. Int J Plant Genomics. 2008, 412696, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1155/2008/412696&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3393">83</a></sup> using the assembled genome sequence as input. Aiming to validate the genome assembly and to assess genetic relationships within the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> complex, primers flanking each SSR were designed using the software Primer3 2.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Koressaar, T. &amp; Remm, M. Enhancements and modifications of primer design program Primer3. Bioinformatics. 23, 1289â1291 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3403">84</a></sup>. Fifteen randomly selected primer pairs (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S9</a>) were synthesized and amplified in genomic DNA from 10 <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> genotypes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#MOESM1">S12</a>). SSRs previously developed for <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> were also tested<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Garbus, I. et al. De novo transcriptome sequencing and assembly from apomictic and sexual Eragrostis curvula genotypes. PloS one. 12, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0185595&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3426">9</a></sup> to increase the number of markers included in the phylogenetic tree, thus improving the accuracy of the results. PCR reactions used 1âÎ¼l of 10âmM dNTPs mix, 1Ã reaction buffer, 0.5âÎ¼l of each forward and reverse primer (100âpmol/Î¼l), 1.5âU Taq DNA polymerase and 60âng of template genomic DNA in 20âÎ¼l of reaction volume. The PCR program consisted of an initial DNA denaturation at 94âÂ°C for 3âmin, followed by 40 cycles at 94âÂ°C for 30âs at the optimal annealing temperature for each primer pair and 72âÂ°C for 30âs and a final extension of 5âmin at 72âÂ°C. The amplicons were validated in Victoria cultivar using 1.5% agarose gels and the presence/absence of the bands was assessed trough 6% polyacrylamide gels. To size the bands the ladder used was the Genebiotech 100âbp Plus DNA (L00307P).</p><p>The similarity of the cultivars and the relative position of cv. Victoria within the <i>E</i>. <i>curvula</i> complex were assessed regarding the presence or absence of individual bands in the polyacrylamide gels obtained from each primer pair in each individual tested. The pairwise distance of this binary (1 presence, 0 absence) data matrix was computed using the Jaccard method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Hamers, L. Similarity measures in scientometric research: The Jaccard index versus Saltonâs cosine formula. Inf Process Manag. 3, 315â18 (1989)." href="/articles/s41598-019-46610-0#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d60274e3439">85</a></sup> and the phylogenetic tree was created with hierarchical clustering and the ward D2 method in an R environment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Data Access</h3><p>Under the NCBI bioproject PRJNA508722 (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/</a>) are available:</p><p>-genome sequences</p><p>-genome annotation</p><p>-DArT reads and markers sequences</p></div></div></section>
